  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_e368f5e7-18ff-4a1c-94e8-9fc09a1c7142'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

Mais voulez-vous me donner une signature ?<br />Machinalement, Ève se lève, se penche sur le registre et signe.<br />- Parfait, conclut la vieille dame.     <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3271485220?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=nobels">nobels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3271485220" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10071689766949675937">Hope dies last... or doesn't die at all...<br/> <br/>... as evidenced by the fact that I started to read a novel by SARTRE and found myself hoping for a happier end than the sad, murderous beginning (I was going to exclaim: "What was I thinking?" only to realise that the answer must be: "Not that much!"). Hoping for Sartre to give me hope is the most hopelessly optimistic thing I can do, I guess. So of course I will be disappointed. Sartre, after all, never disappoints in that respect. <br/><br/>Can you get your li</span>
  <span id="freeText10071689766949675937" style="display:none">Hope dies last... or doesn't die at all...<br /> <br />... as evidenced by the fact that I started to read a novel by SARTRE and found myself hoping for a happier end than the sad, murderous beginning (I was going to exclaim: "What was I thinking?" only to realise that the answer must be: "Not that much!"). Hoping for Sartre to give me hope is the most hopelessly optimistic thing I can do, I guess. So of course I will be disappointed. Sartre, after all, never disappoints in that respect. <br /><br />Can you get your life right if you are given the chance to live again? <br /><br />Nah!<br /><br />But living means trying against all odds, hoping and hoping from one disappointment to the next. <br /><br />As long as there is life, there is hope. How true, and how sadly unhelpful as long as life keeps letting hope down for no other reason than because it can. We want to be wound up and suffer from the suspense created by hope and and its stepsister fear, apparently. Why else do we go on like we do, meddling with our happiness in HOPE of something else? Doom is in the fact that happy can be turned into a comparative and a superlative. Happier and happiest make happy look undesirable.<br /><br />Les jeux sont faits, I close my eyes and hope ... hope ... hope ... <br /><br />And then, when the die is cast, life turns into: "if only..."<br /><br />Sartre knew his game, and his imaginary life in the underworld means eternal repetition of human folly for no other reason than that time does not end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10071689766949675937" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3333823137092102088" style="display:none">YOLO! <div id="review_73247981" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73247981" />
    <a title="Kristin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2264362-kristin"><img alt="Kristin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1354174602p2/2264362.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/73247981?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristin" href="/user/show/2264362-kristin">Kristin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73247981" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11394829881906783946">This was my first experience with Sartre's works of fiction. There aren't many philosophers who can pull off an engaging story quite like Sartre. The novel is short and a quick-read. The story tells of a man and woman who meet and fall in love in the afterlife, and are given a chance to rejoin the living for 24hrs and prove it. if they succeed, they get a second shot at life. if not, it's back to oblivion with them. <br/><br/>It's no surprise that the story is philosophically compelling, but the weight of</span>
  <span id="freeText11394829881906783946" style="display:none">This was my first experience with Sartre's works of fiction. There aren't many philosophers who can pull off an engaging story quite like Sartre. The novel is short and a quick-read. The story tells of a man and woman who meet and fall in love in the afterlife, and are given a chance to rejoin the living for 24hrs and prove it. if they succeed, they get a second shot at life. if not, it's back to oblivion with them. <br /><br />It's no surprise that the story is philosophically compelling, but the weight of it's impact actually has me re-evaluating life priorities. I am in love with this story and that hasn't happened in a long time. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11394829881906783946" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2302178343" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9236327940996652647">This is the second book I’ve read of the same author. <br/>It has two protagonists which a death would occur to them. One by her husband the other by his rival related to the political reunions. <br/>It reflexes the life after death, what would it be like. Without any religious incoming opinions, from such a pure idea. <br/>Death was there told as a thing that you would lost yourself, your last self, when you can’t see your reflex in the mirror. No certain explanation about your existence. <br/>A death which they c</span>
  <span id="freeText9236327940996652647" style="display:none">This is the second book I’ve read of the same author. <br />It has two protagonists which a death would occur to them. One by her husband the other by his rival related to the political reunions. <br />It reflexes the life after death, what would it be like. Without any religious incoming opinions, from such a pure idea. <br />Death was there told as a thing that you would lost yourself, your last self, when you can’t see your reflex in the mirror. No certain explanation about your existence. <br />A death which they call you by your name. <br />A death which you are in your most liberty. <br />The dead people will get a chance to see what will happen after they lose their lives, although they prefer to escape from that scenes most of them. They get use to forget about their past. <br />Finally the thing that the story was rolling on was that the two protagonistas whom they were died, they start to feel that they were made for each other. At the end they got the chance to live again but this time for finding themselves as lovers. <br />Unfortunately and apparently they found themselves but at the last mins they gave up to the “love” promise they made to each other. They again spend the time and opportunity to solve the problems they were struggling at the moment of their death. Surely the society problems. <br />At my point: the pure love is unreachable and there is no place for it in comparison with the society related things (managing a political union, fight for the money that was once yours, rescuing the others lives...)<br />Moreover thanks to the translator “Hossein Kasmaee” for extra information in the introduction section of the book.<br />Is a great book!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9236327940996652647" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8578177899005215211">I had forgotten how crisp and clean Sartre's French can be. Sartre makes his point (several times), of course, because he really wants to tell us something rather than show us something (just in case we don't "get it," I suppose). Love certainly is easier after death because, while we are alive, things just get in the way. Even if we are given a second chance. We make our choices and have to live with them.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5575269166607305504">This was the first book I read by Sartre. It is probably still my favorite. I can't tell you why exactly, I think it was the glimmer of hope that shines in the background of this novel, at least until the end. Said hope is completely absent in everything of his I've read since. Also, the movie made from this book is truly wonderful in its way.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10441426221324859098">My French teacher was pretty rubbish at actually teaching us French, but I will never forget the couple of weeks we spent with her reading us this story and us scrambling along with the dictionary to keep up because it was so captivating. It's never really let me go since and every once in a while I poke at my French copy of it and scramble along with the dictionary as much now as I did back then.   <span id="freeText10441426221324859098" style="display:none">My French teacher was pretty rubbish at actually teaching us French, but I will never forget the couple of weeks we spent with her reading us this story and us scrambling along with the dictionary to keep up because it was so captivating. It's never really let me go since and every once in a while I poke at my French copy of it and scramble along with the dictionary as much now as I did back then. This is the first time I've read a translation of it, and oddly it didn't feel as if I actually missed much when I did try it in French. It's sort of transcendent that way. I love how simple yet evocative the language is. I love what it has to say about love - how it's ultimately on the pessimistic side of realism, but still leaves you with a sliver of hope. The writing is so sparse it's pared down to the very bones of human emotion, but still shows you how the bones are beautiful. </span>

          </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10930141430710720480">I read this book in French for my intermediate French class at UCR. At first, I was unhappy that my teacher picked a book by Sartre because I had read "Nausea" during my first year of college and hadn't enjoyed it. I'm glad to say that I enjoyed this book far more than "Nausea."<br/><br/>This was really well written. Pierre and Eve were such round characters and I loved them both. There were moments in the story where I caught my breath, especially the scene when Pierre and Eve are together in his bedroom</span>
  <span id="freeText10930141430710720480" style="display:none">I read this book in French for my intermediate French class at UCR. At first, I was unhappy that my teacher picked a book by Sartre because I had read "Nausea" during my first year of college and hadn't enjoyed it. I'm glad to say that I enjoyed this book far more than "Nausea."<br /><br />This was really well written. Pierre and Eve were such round characters and I loved them both. There were moments in the story where I caught my breath, especially the scene when Pierre and Eve are together in his bedroom (so romantic!). I loved the conflict between love and duty, morality and desire. The ending was so sad, but Sartre succeeded in getting his point across. The bittersweetness was so fitting.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer739304934" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer336202446310872893">Prose text that looks like a film scenario: actions and feelings are described rather 'clinically'. The storyline is fairly thin: two very different people die, meet in the hereafter, get a second chance, but especially social differences and obligations drive them apart again. The elaboration is cleverly done, but the rapprochement between the two after their death is rather forced.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2188984774141790218">Powerful and symbolic... Satre's mastery of the art of mixed feelings oozes through the lines of the two protagonists of this piece. I couldn't help but desire to push on and find out where things would go for Eve Charlier, a staunchly bourgeoise socialite and Pierre Dumaine, a working-class revolutionary from literally the other side of town. They meet in death and are given a second chance, provided they pass a life-defining test to challenge what their entire lives had been prior to meeting. </span>
  <span id="freeText2188984774141790218" style="display:none">Powerful and symbolic... Satre's mastery of the art of mixed feelings oozes through the lines of the two protagonists of this piece. I couldn't help but desire to push on and find out where things would go for Eve Charlier, a staunchly bourgeoise socialite and Pierre Dumaine, a working-class revolutionary from literally the other side of town. Beautiful and absurd all at once.<br /></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2636107661" />
    <a title="Debbi Mack" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/334550-debbi-mack"><img alt="Debbi Mack" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487003688p2/334550.jpg" /></a>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9589716656212539981">Best story I've read in a long, long time.<br/><br/>The book is written like a screenplay or stage play (third person, present tense), which makes it feel so immediate.<br/><br/>Anyone who's familiar with existentialism won't be too surprised by how things end up. But that doesn't take away from its greatness to read in the least.<br/><br/>Highly recommended.</span>

          </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer480198720895486221">Incredible. Was not disappointed. This strangely gave me comfort in a way. It’s disturbing and terrifying and hopeful all while being predictable (esp if you know your existentialists) . It’s clear that it won’t have a good ending but that just made it so much better. <br/>I laughed out loud at the random shoutout to Camus in the scene at the office. All in all incredible!</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JO3Dg5yONcyet+AyQn2XQiyyfsDJlsNNZptkKI5lurvjb50mR15oA9wHA9kmSWfbNqwTsyvBjooaZ0uSiwJLZA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

As he and her family become friendly, he realizes they are haunted by an old, dark secret . . . but he can’t help falling deeply in love with Katya.Jean-Marc is warned by Katya’s brother that she is delicate and that he should curb his attentions, but he is young, hopeful, and in love . . . and he is certain that Katya returns his affections. When Jean-Marc learns that the Trevilles are planning to leave the village forever, he insists on a final meeting with Katya. That meeting and the events that follow turn what was an idyllic romance into an unending nightmare. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6588788519062288965"><b>Jean-Paul Charles Aymard Sartre</b>, normally known simply as Jean-Paul Sartre, was a French existentialist philosopher and pioneer, dramatist and screenwriter, novelist and critic. He was a leading figure in 20th century French philosophy.<br/><br/>He declined the award of the 1964 Nobel Prize in Literature "for his work which, rich in ideas and filled with the spirit of freedom and the quest for truth, has ex</span>
  <span id="freeText6588788519062288965" style="display:none"><b>Jean-Paul Charles Aymard Sartre</b>, normally known simply as Jean-Paul Sartre, was a French existentialist philosopher and pioneer, dramatist and screenwriter, novelist and critic. He was a leading figure in 20th century French philosophy.<br /><br />He declined the award of the 1964 Nobel Prize in Literature "for his work which, rich in ideas and filled with the spirit of freedom and the quest for truth, has exerted a far-reaching influence on our age."<br /><br />In the years around the time of his death, however, existentialism declined in French philosophy and was overtaken by structuralism, represented by Levi-Strauss and, one of Sartre's detractors, Michel Foucault.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6588788519062288965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1171412-les-jeux-sont-faits">Quotes from De teerling is ge...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Le vieillard: Maintenant... vous voyez aussi les morts.<br />Pierre: Comment les distingue-t-on des vivants?<br />Le vieillard: C'est bien simple: les vivants, eux, sonts toujours pressés.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/9045575-le-vieillard-maintenant-vous-voyez-aussi-les-morts-pierre-comment">1 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Bald sind sie allein auf der Tanzfläche, und Pierre führt seine Partnerin schon viel sicherer.<br />“Was haben sie mir denn da vorgemacht?” sagt Ève. “Sie tanzen doch sehr gut.”<br />“Das ist das erste Mal, dass man mir das sagt.”<br />“Sie brauchten eben mich als Tänzerin.”<br />“Ich glaube es fast …”<br />Sie sehen sich an und tanzen eine Weile schweigend.<br />“Sagen Sie”, fragte Pierre plötzlich, “was geht hier eigentlich vor? Vorhin dachte ich nur an meine Sorgen, und jetzt bin ich hier … Ich tanze und sehe nur Ihr Lächeln … Wenn das der Tod … wäre …”<br />“Das?”<br />“Ja. Mit Ihnen tanzen, immer, nichts sehen als Sie, alles andere vergessen …”<br />“Ja, und?”<br />“Der Tod wäre besser als das Leben. Finden sie nicht auch?”<br />“Halten Sie mich fester”, haucht sie.<br />Ihre Gesichter sind einander ganz nahe. Sie tanzen noch einen Augenblick weiter, und sie wiederholt:<br />“Halten sie mich fester…”<br />Plötzlich wird Pierres Gesicht traurig. Er hört auf zu tanzen, rückt ein wenig von Ève ab und murmelt:<br />“Es ist ja alles Theater. Ich habe Ihre Taille nicht einmal berührt …”<br />Ève begreift nun ebenfalls:<br />“Wahrhaftig”, sagt sie langsam, “wir tanzen jeder für sich …”<br />Sie bleiben voreinander stehen.<br />Dann streckt Pierre die Hände aus, als wolle er sie auf die Schultern der jungen Frau legen, dann zieht er sie unwillig wieder zurück:<br />“Mein Gott”, sagt er, “wie süß wäre es, Ihre Schultern zu berühren. Ich möchte so gerne Ihren Atem spüren, wenn Sie mich anlächeln. Aber auch das habe ich verpasst. Ich bin ihnen zu spät begegnet …”<br />Ève legt Pierre die Hand auf die Schulter.<br />Sie sieht ihn liebevoll an:<br />“Ich gäbe meine Seele dafür hin, einen Augenblick lang wieder zu leben und mit Ihnen zu tanzen.”<br />“Ihre Seele?”<br />“Das ist alles, was wir noch besitzen.”<br />Pierre nähert sich seiner Begleiterin und umfasst sie von neuem. Sie beginnen wieder zu tanzen, sehr zart, Wange an Wange, mit geschlossenen Augen.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/1325457-bald-sind-sie-allein-auf-der-tanzfl-che-und-pierre-f-hrt">0 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/1171412-les-jeux-sont-faits">More quotes&hellip;</a>
